Harry Potter Page to Screen: The Complete Filmmaking Journey is a behemoth of a book, and I had not expected it to be this huge (not that I'm complaining). It is an oversized hardcover with an embossed photograph of Hogwarts on the cover. It looks like a magickal volume and in a way it is, for it captures all of the magic of the Harry Potter movies, from Harry Potter and the Sorcerer's Stone all the way to Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows, Part Two. Any self-avowed fan of the HP stories and movies will treasure this beautiful volume filled with movie lore, and many more.<br /><br />The first color photograph one comes across as one flips the book open is a full size portrait of Albus Dumbledore, Headmaster of Hogwarts (Michael Gambon, who played Dumbledore in HP films 3-8). His face expresses both concern and puzzlement, and it is almost as if he is there to welcome readers on a journey of knowledge and discovery which is what this book ultimately rewards the reader with. Flip to the next page, and one's attention is captured by a double-page (full spread) painting of Hogwarts, majestic in all its splendor, though details are rather fuzzy. There is also a fold-out spread of the main cast in movies 1-8 - the actors portraying Dumbledore, Hagrid, Hermione, Harry, Ron, Snape, Lucius Malfoy, Prof McGonagall, Luna Lovegood, Ginny Weasley, the twins Fred and George, the Dark Lord, and many more.<br /><br />So, the contents...the book (531 pages) is divided into three parts:<br />Part I: The Making of Harry Potter<br />Introduction<br />Setting the Scene<br />HP and the Sorcerer's Stone<br />HP and the Chamber of Secrets<br />HP and the Prisoner of Azkaban<br />HP and the Goblet of Fire<br />HP and the Order of the Phoenix<br />HP and the Half-Blood Prince<br />HP and the Deathly Hallows<br /><br />Part II: The Art of Harry Potter<br />Introduction<br />Characters: Costuming<br />Locations: Set Design<br />Creatures: Special Makeup and Digital Effects<br />Artifacts: Prop Making<br /><br />Part III: Epilogue<br />The Golden Boards<br />Acknowledgements and Colophon<br /><br />The book goes in-depth into the many aspects of the film-making process, including valuable insights from and about the directors, actors, and all the other significant people who have worked on the movies, not forgetting the author of course. If you've ever wondered how the cast was put together, the process by which the directors for the movies were picked, etc. then this wonderful book answers all these questions and more. These details are enhanced by the hundreds of photographs that are found throughout the book, and what makes these pictures all the more extraordinary and valuable to the discerning reader is the fact that many of them were taken on set, during the making of the films, and apart from the photographs there are also full-color paintings in glorious detail. As a dragon fan myself, I was positively enraptured by the concept art of Paul Gatling depicting the mighty Hungarian Horntail in all its wondrous glory, which dare I say surpassed even the beauty of the dragon depicted on film? These are not minuscule drawings, but large-scale drawings in fold-out form. Other magical creatures get their share in this book such as the centaurs, dear old Buckbeak, the thestrals, the dementors, the process of transformation of Remus Lupin into a werewolf and many more. Be still my beating heart!<br /><br />Remember all those newspaper headlines from The Daily Prophet, the Quibbler editions, and the Wanted Posters? They are depicted here in detail and also in the case of The Quibbler, in color! Ever wondered about the inspiration for those Death Eater's Masks? Its covered here in full-color. The significant places in the world of Harry Potter - Hogwarts, Diagon Alley (loved the map), the Burrow, Hagrid's Hut, the Forbidden Forest, and many more are also given descriptions and explanations in this magnificent volume.<br /><br/>As I continue reading through this tome, I have found some little things to gripe about - certain characters, albeit minor ones, are not really given any coverage, just a mere mention. Remember the ghost that haunts the abandoned toilet in Chamber of Secrets, i.e. Moaning Myrtle? Just a mention and that's it. I would have loved to see at least some description of the filming of the bathroom scene in HP and the Goblet of Fire in which Moaning Myrtle makes another appearance and helps Harry puzzle out the riddle, but no such luck. The book does however go into detail about the second task and the glorious underwater lake scene in Goblet of Fire, and how the lifecasts were conceived and made. But poor Hedwig gets left out here - except for a brief mention on p.238 and concept art depicting Harry and Hedwig overlooking Hogwarts.<br /><br />Btw, for those who are wondering about how this compares to [[ASIN:0061997811 Harry Potter Film Wizardry]], I'd say they are both complementary (I own a copy of HP Film Wizardry) to each other. While Page to Screen provides more details on the movie-making process and covers just about everything (I say just about as there are some things not covered), Film Wizardry provides more of an overview with many fun elements thrown in for devoted fans such as the various foldouts and inserts. For example, in HP Film Wizardry, readers will get a chance to peruse a copy of the Marauder's Map, Harry's acceptance letter to Hogwarts, a catalogue from Borgin and Burke's, stickers in the Advanced Potion-Making pamphlet, two boxes of treats (sans the treats) from Honeydukes of Hogsmeade (you can tear them out and fold them into actual treat boxes, not that I would!), a Quidditch World Cup Programme, a copy of the Yule Ball Programme, Educational Decree No. 29, a product catalogue of Weasley's Wizard Wheezes, and a Ministry of Magic ID card. Such goodies are not to be found in Page to Screen, so I'd definitely recommend getting both books. I've frequently found myself reading both together, and comparing notes.<br /><br />My one complaint here is the absence of an Index - how could they have produced such a wonderful companion book to the movies and not have an index to help readers look up any detail that springs to mind? I can't fathom it, but I envision myself spending lots of quality time enjoying the delights of this book, so I shall not let this trouble me too much:) In fact, I am sorely tempted to purchase another book to keep as a collector's item as I can see myself thumbing through this copy until it becomes dog-eared! This is a beautiful volume of substance for any fan of the HP books and movies./>As I continue reading through this tome, I have found some little things to gripe about - certain characters, albeit minor ones, are not really given any coverage, just a mere mention. B005WTR4ZI|4.0|Cross posted from my blog.  I've just read Brynjolfsson and McAfee's Race against the Machine in one sitting when I have masses of other pressing stuff to do.  It is short, sharp, engaging and easy to read. Put down that Scandinavian crime novel, ignore your travel expense application issues and read this book instead. I'm perhaps reading too much into the title,  but I can't help wondering if it isn't a hat tip to the rock band Rage Against the Machine.  If it is, deeply nifty sub-editing coolness.  If not, it is a lovely  unintended consequence.  The book highlights the accelerating disruption that technology brings to the workplace and to the very definition of work. There is dark side to technology, and the authors have done a nuanced job in exploring this.  It makes a worthwhile change from the technology=progress drum beat.  It was especially good to see a section on the growing gap between wage  and productivity growth.  To see disquiet about median wage stagnation from technology focused researchers is a very fine thing.  There is more than a whiff of valorization in their argument.  Brynjolfsson and McAfee make excellent use of statistics, and this work is no exception. They use numbers to illuminate, and they do it well. The Bill Gates in a bar story is a lovely explanation of mean and median. They explain, but don't condescend.  As with much of US business academia, the book is centred on the US economy, with fleeting mentions of the rest of world.  I didn't spot the dreaded phrase \\"Corporate America\\", but it may have been lurking there. In particular the solution section was too US focused. Moaning about  H-1B visas etc... However suggestions 17,18, 19 are spot on.  17. Reduce the large implicit and explicit subsidies to financial services. This sector attracts a disproportionate number of the best and the brightest minds and technologies, in part because the government effectively guarantees \\"too big to fail\\" institutions.   18. Reform the patent system. Not only does it take years to issue good patents due to the backlog and shortage of qualified examiners, but too many low-quality patents are issued, clogging our courts. As a result, patent trolls are chilling innovation rather than encouraging it.  19. Shorten, rather than lengthen, copyright periods and increase the flexibility of fair use.  Copyright covers too much digital content. Rather than encouraging innovation, as specified in the Constitution, excessive restrictions like the Sonny Bono Copyright Term Extension Act inhibit mixing and matching of content and using it creatively in new ways.  There are strong echoes of Larry Lessig in the IP section (as an aside I'd like to get the authors' views of Lessig's recent work on political corruption).  More broadly though I'd like to see business school academia and IT research engaging more with the rich research tapestry of sociology and political philosophy, how about more Jessop and Harvey, and Herbert Marcusse needs a serious dust off.  I fancy I heard the very faint clang of  Weber's iron cage in this work. I'd suggest that Maslow and maybe Hayek can take a rest for a while.  This book is excellent,  but would have been seminal if it had built upon the work of that chap from Trier.

Ahead of the actual thesis is a collection of Marx's notes from Lucretius made in Latin, as well as extensive notes from various sources in Greek. 1423389387|4.0|I am torn about this book series.<br /><br />I loved -- LOVED -- The Name Of The Wind. I picked that book up when the various internet sources I monitor wouldn't stop talking about it, and boy were they right. It is sort of a cross between J.K. Rowling's Harry Potter series (but, for adults), L.E. Modesitt's Magic of Recluse series (with all of the details on building things, and making things), and Terry Goodkind's Sword of Truth Series (for the violence, death and destruction aspect of fantasy).<br /><br />--minor spoiler alert past this point for non-main plot points--<br /><br />And, see ... the Terry Goodkind aspect of it, is what is souring me on the series.  I would love to give this book 5 stars. But I just can't.  Things were going more or less well, until very near the end. At that point, Kvothe has picked up some serious fighting skills, and is on his way back to his most recent point of origin, when he encounters a group impersonating his people -- and they have kidnapped two teenaged girls, and are raping them daily as a group. He is offered \\"use\\" of the girls, as a fee for joining them.<br /><br />Kvothe kills every last one of them, and saves the girls.<br /><br />Now, don't get me wrong, they needed a good killing. I am in favor of the death penalty for all rapists of any size, shape, color, nationality, culture or ethnicity. Preferably in the most painful way possible.<br /><br />The killing of the rapists isn't what I object to. What I object to is the alluding to rape. That was the same horrifying garbage in Goodkind's basically-slash fiction, that made me toss his book, and never look back.  To Rothfuss' credit, he never depicts the actual rape, and he doesn't depict it as sexy, or that the girls were in any way enjoying it -- as some authors sometimes do. He depicts it honestly, as the horrifying thing it is, and pulls no punches that these are two, young, innocent girls (about 15/16) who are being tortured by monsters.  There is no mercy or pity in me for rapists. The subject brings up a horrified rage in me like no other subject on earth.<br /><br />If there is anything in this world that horrifies me the most, it is rape and torture of females young and old. It makes me physically ill just to read about it in the mild format that Rothfuss presented the topic in.  I don't understand why Kvothe couldn't have happened across them right after they had kidnapped the girls, but they hadn't harmed them yet? It presents Kvothe with the same situation to be furious about, and the same two girls to rescue, and the same subhumans to exterminate, but it saves the girls from having to suffer. This is fantasy escapist literature. Emphasis on ESCAPIST. If I wanted horrifying stories, I would read the world news. Everyone knows there are monsters in the world, I don't feel any particular need to read about them in my escapist fantasy novels.<br /><br />I do not agree with the point of view some authors have that they need to shock their audience in order to get them to respect their work. If we all think back, we all remember plenty of books quite fondly from our childhood and teen years which didn't use shock value to sell themselves. J.K. Rowling is the most recent example of such an author who will present adult topics, but in a way that doesn't go out of its way to horrify the reader. There were also books like the Hardy Boys, Nancy Drew, Clive Cussler's Dirk Pitt adventures, etc. All of those (and countless others) present thrills and adventure, and even wonder and magic, but do so without using shock value, and/or horrifying the reader.<br /><br />The writing of Rothfuss' books is 5 star quality. His first book was very well done.  The overall story being told in these books is also spectacular. Even though I know Kvothe isn't going to die, and how he is going to end up (due to the way that the story is being told), that doesn't lessen my excitement for what happens next, nor temper my dread when he is in peril.The first book, The Name Of The Wind, was brutal -- but it wasn't horrifying. Kvothe was put through some SERIOUS trials, and he had to witness some hard things, but we get through it with him as we read along.  In this book, The Wise Man's Fear, I would say about 95% of it is similar to the tone of the first book. It is just the one encounter with the rapists that soured it for me.  Earlier in the book when Kvothe was<br /><br />---MAJOR spoiler alert---<br /><br />learning about love and lust from none other than Felurian herself, and then getting her to aid him with a magical cloak, and knowledge that few mortals possessed, I was on the edge of my seat! Also, his handling of the bandits, the encounter with his old foe, the learning of the secret he learned while in the Fay realm (it is really hard to write this without just flat out giving things away), and his adventures back at the University all had me anxious to learn what happened next, and satisfied as a reader.<br /><br />---END major spoilers---<br /><br />So, if rape and rapists don't horrify you like they do me, then this is a 5 star book. If they do, skip chapters 30 through 34 (trust me), and it is a 4 star book. I really really wish that Rothfuss had NOT added those scenes, but he did, and that kind of ruined my enjoyment of the book. I will still probably get book 3, but I may have someone else read it for me first this time, and let me know what chapters to skip.ut through some SERIOUS trials, and he had to witness some hard things, but we get through it with him as we read along. No doubt H20 would have been greatly enhanced if John Carpenter had directed.  Here's some of why this film didn't cut the mustard:  The absence of logic is insulting to the intelligent viewer:  Laurie Strode supposedly faked her death 20 years ago and has now assumed the alias of Keri Tate in order to hide herself from her brother.  This makes for a fairly implausible script.    Characters are (extremely) much less interesting than the original.  Suspense scenes do not build enough and are therefore less than stellar.  Some scenes such as when the keys dropped were downright silly and annoying.  Decapitation scene simply didn't look real.  The soundtrack is disappointing and doesn't really help the movie.   Michael's mask and eyes were less scary than earlier movies.  Michael could have been killed by repeatedly bashing in his head with a rock.  Instead the young lady just gives him one quick crack to the noggin and runs.

I can't recall ever seeing another band enjoy their time onstage together as much as Rush does, and they've been at it for nearly 40 years (current line-up).<br /><br />Neil's drum solo occurs about midway through the second half of the show, and despite some slight inconsistency in the rhythm of his hi-hats (an observation, not a criticism) he delivers on the promise of his reputation as one of rock's finest drummers.  He once again ends with a nod to the Big Band era.<br /><br />Geddy's bass work still impresses:  his improvisational \\"fills\\" sound intricate and effortless at the same time.  Too bad his vocals get a little buried in the sound mix (unless that was due to limitations of the movie theater's sound system).<br /><br />With the concept videos, the concert runs darn close to 3 hours -- but never drags for even a moment. B002Q9MZF6|5.0|An opera film is vastly different from a recorded live performance, and it is impossible to compare the two.  Each has its own peculiarities and appeal.<br /><br />Robert Dornhelm's film of \\"La Boh&egrave;me\\" is amazingly good.  As a favourite opera, I've seen and heard many versions, and did not really expect to find anything \\"new\\" in this one.  How wrong I was!  At first viewing, it seemed as if I caught my breath at the start and didn't let it go until the last note died with Mimi.<br /><br />The sets are realistic and evocative of the time period and the changing seasons that underline the story.  The attention to little details is wonderful ... the rough table manners of the hungry men, the way Rodolfo fusses over the faint Mimi, his visible \\"gulp\\" as her closeness begins to affect him, the good natured teasing he receives from the others at Momus, the warming of Mimi's medicine in the final scene.<br /><br />Anna Netrebko's singing is just glorious.  Her Mimi is different from most - ill she may be, but she is certainly not helpless or emotionally fragile.  This is a girl who probably knows she doesn't have a long future, and is therefore determined to make the most of every day.  She initiates the \\"candle\\" episode, and when love comes, she seizes it.<br /><br />The Rodolfo of Rolando Villaz&oacute;n is a young man caught in a whirlwind of unexpected events - the almost unbelievable coming of love to his life, the inevitable possessiveness, the torment of his inability to save Mimi's life with his love, the ultimate despair.  He looks the part, acts the part superbly, and his wonderful voice carries his heart (and mine!) on its waves.<br /><br />This film was my first experience of the compelling artistic partnership of Mr Villaz&oacute;n and Ms Netrebko, and I can certainly see why they received such acclaim. \\"O Soave Fanciulla\\" simply took my breath away ... and from the director's comments in the bonus documentary, that reaction was not unique to me.<br /><br />The supporting cast members are likewise believable in every way - I was especially impressed with the Musetta of Nicole Cabell. It enables us to see firsthand the challenges of creating a successful operatic story when filming the scenes devoid of a live audience, orchestra and stage.  A professional reviewer in an Australian arts magazine recently labelled this \\"the finest opera film of the last two decades\\".  I agree with him.

1451648537|5.0|INTRODUCTION  Apple has always meant more to me than as a computer company, because of my early experiences in the late 1970's and early 1980's from age 8 using the Apple ][, //e, and later the Mac.  They represented amazing products that I could understand even as a child, that this was the direction of the future.  It was odd to me then, that the world was still embracing the MS-DOS command line interface and the IBM PC/AT machines.  When in the late 1990's, Apple neared bankruptcy, with Microsoft Windows dominating the market, it taught me as a young man that companies that try to make the very best can be under appreciated by the masses, just as the adults near me in the 1980's could not see the amazing nature of my Apple //e and Mac back then.  Good guys, it seemed, do finish last.  It was disheartening.  Since the return of Steve Jobs to Apple, the world now knows of his genius and brilliance.  This biography is utterly amazing.  I could not stop reading the entire biography and finished in less than 2 days.  WHAT I LIKED  1. Extraordinarily comprehensive - The book covers an immense number of different \\"phases\\" of his life from his famous adoption story to the start of Apple Computer, to NeXt, Pixar, love life, development of his iconic products, to the time before his death (although his death is actually never mentioned).  2. Ruthlessly objective - As a fan of Steve Jobs, I cringed at all the negative descriptions of Jobs's conduct with strangers, his management team, other CEO's, etc.  I knew of his candor and lack of sensitivity towards others, but the degree to which this is depicted made me cringe and even wonder if Jobs should not be garnering so much world-wide respect.  This sentiment was strong in the beginning of the biography, but by the end of the biography, I had actually become accustomed to Jobs's personality through the biography, almost as if I had personally known the man and adapted to him.  The biography actually made me feel like I knewhim.  3. Extraordinary historical perspective - Even if this biography were not to mention Steve Jobs, it would be fascinating.  There is so much written about the history of Silicon Valley, other famous CEOs, musicians, artists, politicians, etc, that the book is enticing.  4. Extraordinary perspective on other famous leaders - Jobs spoke candidly about his opinions regarding virtually every important person that may have crossed his path.  There are comments and stories regarding John Sculley, President Clinton, Obama, Bill Gates, Jeffrey Katzenberg (Disney), Michael Eisner (Disney), Bob Iger (Disney), Bono, Joan Baez, Bob Dylan, Yoko Ono, Steve Wozniak, Larry Ellison (Oracle), Eric Schmidt (Google), Larry Page (Google), Andy Grove (Intel), etc.  5. Extremely detailed descriptions of Jobs's business decision-making processes - This is true throughout the biography, but especially so towards the last third, where there is an extraordinarily detailed account by Jobs of his thought process during development of the iPod, iTunes, iPhone, iPad, and iCloud.  In this latter third of the biography, whatever doubt may have existed of whether or not Jobs should be so revered is laid to rest when we witness his amazing decision-making ability.  6. Unexpectedly funny - Especially in the very beginning of the biography, you can't help but laugh when you read about John Sculley's first day at Apple and seeing Jobs sitting on a desk playing with his bare toes.  7. Jobs's personal life - This has always been an enigma and the most many knew of Jobs's personal life came from his 2005 Stanford commencement speech.  We see into his early girlfriends' perspectives of Jobs, his current wife and children's perspective.  The fascinating story of his biological parents, biological sister, daughter for whom he initially denied custody, three children and wife.  There is much written about his perspective on Zen Buddhism and his trek to India.  8. Extremely detailed - For all the above points, there was an immense amount of detail that I never envisioned would exist in this biography.  9. Easy to read - The author makes reading each sentence effortless.  WHAT I DIDN'T LIKE  1. Not enough photos - The few photos that were included were great, but it left you wanting more.  2. Possibly too much of the negative aspects of Jobs's personality were described - No doubt that the man could belittle others, but there was so much emphasis of this especially in the beginning of the bio, that I wondered if the author didn't try too hard to make this point for fear of being accused of being too soft in his description of Jobs  3. Some very slight repetition in the very beginning of the biography from passages found in the middle and end of the biography.  4. I wished for more of Steve Jobs's perspective - Every now and then, the author would mention what Jobs thought of a certain past event but I wish there were more of those.  So much of the biography read more like a history book trying to be objective and accurate, but I really wanted to know what Jobs thought about everything.  I wanted his perspective more, even if it would make the book less objective.  CONCLUSION  This biography is amazing because of the subject matter, but it is also well-written.  It seemed to be such an effort at objectiveness, however, that it actually lacked what I wanted to read most, which was Jobs's perspective.  I appreciated the author's efforts and painting an accurate historical picture, but I really wanted to know what was on Jobs's mind regarding everything that was written about him.  There was not enough of that, which is unfortunate, because it is in my opinion, his perspective that mattered the most.

B003CN4R74|4.0|One thing that Evanescence is known for is drama.  Just look at Amy Lee.  She screams drama.  Look at the cover art to their albums.  Drama.  Listen to the way that Amy incorporates that slight operatic edge to her music.  DRAMA.  So, in listening to their latest album I couldn't help but dissect the tracks in terms of dramatic tension.  These songs swim in that realm, and so understanding the focal points of the tracks is interesting and fun.  So, this is an odd review for me but I hope you enjoy it.  I've decided to take each track, one by one, and simply point out the key dramatic moments.<br /><br />Let's start at the beginning.<br /><br />`What You Want' - 2:22 and then again at 2:30 / Lee's vocals combined with the sharp shift in musical composition add to the dramatic weight of the track.<br /><br />`Made of Stone' - the extra drum kick at 1:46 adds a welcomed kick, but the real `moment' is found at 2:10 when Amy's vocals reach that pitch and then at 2:23 when she hits the word \\"remember\\" (possibly my favorite vocal `moment' on the album) and the musical tones shift slightly; it almost becomes operatic in texture and the `drama' is certainly heightened.<br /><br />`The Change' - then entire chorus can constitute as drama, the first of which begins at merely 44 seconds in, but even the milky way in which Amy delivers the verses are drenched in drama.  The vocal cascade at 2:38 that spills into the final chorus is explosive, but the real `moment' for me is the way Amy utters that final \\"enough\\"; the way it bleeds out totally reads `drama'.<br /><br />`My Heart Is Broken' - the piano backing instrumentation is flawlessly atmospheric, and Lee's dragged out words always add dramatic emphasis, but listen to the sudden baritone to adopts for certain words (like the word \\"us\\" at 1:36).  But it's the rise and fall that really elevates the drama here.  The escalation beginning at 3:00, those vocals at 3:07 and then the comedown at 3:21 is brilliantly played to swell beneath the listener's skin.<br /><br />`The Other Side' - the reverberated \\"home\\" at 2:14 is pure drama.<br /><br />`Erase This' - this song is drama from beginning to end, but the way Amy hits the word \\"is\\" at a mere 39 seconds in is just astonishing.  The chorus breakdowns are powerful and stirring, and then it just goes to a whole other level starting with the bridge at 2:12 and then just comes full circle with the bleed-through into the chorus again at 2:33 (well, structure-wise), finishing off the song with perfect dramatic sense.<br /><br />`Lost in Paradise' - this distilled vocals evoke pure drama, especially with Amy's signature operatic overtones, but it is at the musical upswing at 1:51 and then, more effectively, the musical shift at 3:20 that really brings it all together.  All in all, this is one of the most dramatic tracks on the album.<br /><br />`Sick' - the track carries on much like a generic rock track, but the muted \\"sick of it all\\" that starts at 2:42 adds that little touch of flavor; and the chorus itself is pretty aggressive and `dramatic'.<br /><br />`End of the Dream' - just wait for the way she hits \\"dream\\" at 1:27; it's flawless.  Sure, she drags out her words a lot, but the pitch she lathers onto that word at that moment sends shivers.<br /><br />`Oceans' - it's all about that sharp, yet subtle tonal shift at 1:41, that comes and fades into the chorus, but it's enough to grab our attention (I love those little details).  And then there is the musical spiral that begins at 2:21, and that burst after split silence at 2:45 that just takes the drama up another notch.  But she doesn't stop there; you have her fade out of the word \\"hands\\" at 3:21, which is followed by those `oh so serious' violins.<br /><br />`Never Go Back' - the drama begins at a mere 55 seconds in when the second half of the chorus enlists the crunching of guitars and the pleading of Amy's vocals.  It is a nice contrast to the overall speed of the balance of the track; a noticeable dramatic diversion.  2:25 begins the piano work (another tonal shift that is so effective) and that is accompanied by haunting vocals that begin to peak at 2:55, when the violins lend aid and then it all folds into that explosive `second' chorus again at 3:18, rounding out the dramatic tension in the song.<br /><br />`Swimming Home' - the track carries on with no sign of drama, feeling very much like a track Team Sleep would have put together, and then the 2:27 mark comes around and the subtle bounce of lightness fades into a more dramatic sensation as things slow down even more and Amy's vocals start to dwell within that baritone range she pulls out at all the right moments.  It's a small moment, for the track diverts back to the pop tones, but it is enough to make an impression.<br /><br />For me, this is certainly a solid effort.  I think I slightly prefer `The Open Door' to this one, but in all honesty they have really come a long way since their debut and should concentrate on putting out more music (we shouldn't have to wait five years for new music; two years TOPS).  Evanescence can run the risk of sounding a little too similar on some tracks, to where they tend to run together a bit, which is why the closing track is so refreshing because it has such a different sound and scales back the `drama' enough to evoke a different feeling.  Don't get me wrong, I love the drama that Amy and company brings to their work (it's the reason I listen to them) but slight variations do go a long way.<br /><br />And no one can deny that Amy Lee has, possibly, the best female vocals in rock music today (maybe even `music', period) and so hearing her sing is a blessing and a privilege and should be indulged at regular intervals.ed by haunting vocals that begin to peak at 2:55, when the violins lend aid and then it all folds into that explosive `second' chorus again at 3:18, rounding out the dramatic tension in the song.  `Swimming Home' - the track carries on with no sign of drama, feeling very much like a track Team Sleep would have put together, and then the 2:27 mark comes around and the subtle bounce of lightness fades into a more dramatic sensation as things slow down even more and Amy's vocals start to dwell within that baritone range she pulls out at all the right moments. B005LB1MY6|4.0|Xavier de Rosnay and Gaspard Aug&eacute;, the Parisian pair that make up Justice, took the world by storm with their 2007 debut record, \\"Cross.\\" Drawing instant comparisons to the likes of Daft Punk, another electronic dance duo, Justice quickly began selling out arenas and headlining festivals with their heavy hitting beats and instantly catchy hooks.<br /><br />On \\"Audio, Video, Disco,\\" Justice expands upon their successful debut with more elements of rock and roll including songs driven by power chords and electric guitar solos. That is not to say Justice has strayed away from the electronic dance tunes that gave them an audience; far from it. \\"Audio, Video, Disco\\" is still a dance album likely to receive a heavy rotation in clubs around the world, but Justice's sound is more progressive than before and they're formulating a more unique identity for themselves as their sound evolves.<br /><br />For the first minute of the opening track, \\"Horsepower,\\" it sounds as though the song could fit just as easily on a Dream Theater record.<br /><br />Harmonized guitars playing arpeggios, drum fills on the third and fourth beats of every measure, and only hints of a synthesized loop in the distance. The track continues to build rapidly, however, filling the listener with anticipation until an abrupt fade out followed by the Justice entrance everyone was expecting.<br /><br />\\"Horsepower\\" eventually blossoms into one of the most familiar sounding Justice tracks on the entire record. A kick drum pounds the ears on every beat, synthesizer chords filter in and out, and the guitar work that sounded like a progressive rock intro in the beginning quickly blends right in with the rest of this powerful instrumental. If there's one track on this album destined for another commercial spot, it's this one.<br /><br />The biggest hit to come from \\"Cross\\" was \\"D.A.N.C.E.\\" thanks, in part, to an incredibly infectious vocal part. Like their debut, \\"Audio, Video, Disco\\" once again calls on the occasional guest vocal to really get the crowd moving. \\"Civilization\\" leans heavily on British singer Ali Love to carry the melody and although the track is repetitive, the addition of Love's voice makes the recording one of the most memorable of the entire record.<br /><br />Although the more balanced blend of electronica and rock and roll sets Justice apart from many other electronic groups and DJs, there are still certain elements of their rock side that feel less original. The guitar work on \\"Newlands\\" is exceptionally reminiscent of much of AC/DC's catalog and the \\"We Will Rock You\\" style stomps and claps of \\"Parade\\" are not even trying to hide their similarities to Queen, but the end result is still enjoyable and proof that Justice has plenty left in store for the world.<br /><br />Similar Artists: Daft Punk, Deadmau5<br /><br />Track Suggestion: \\"Horsepower\\". \\"Civilization\\" leans heavily on British singer Ali Love to carry the melody and although the track is repetitive, the addition of Love's voice makes the recording one of the most memorable of the entire record. B005D0RDKW|4.0|I saw the Blu-Ray of this show last night.  This was projected using a Panasonic theater-grade projector (the logo came up before the showtime) and the Blu Ray was the consumer version that will be released in December (I know this because the menu came up before the showtime).  This review will be based on what I saw, and since I have no control over how that theater calibrated their projector, I cannot say with certainty if any of the flaws were due to the Blu Ray itself, or the projector/sound system.  But here goes.  Sound-wise, things don't start out that impressively, but get progressively better as the show progressed.  At first the sound was a bit flat in terms of dynamics and a bit soft.  The bass lacked punch, and I didn't feel like I was at the show, but watching a show.  The sound was clean, I could hear no feedback or hiss, and I could hear the spaces between the sounds.  Eventually the sound mixers at the concert must have made some corrections because the sound improved from \\"okay\\" to \\"very good\\", at least for a live concert.  There wasn't any obnoxious use of sound imaging, just some ambient crowd noise, nothing flying from one speaker to the next.  Instrumentally, Geddy's bass and Alex's guitars sounded the best--dynamic, never shrill or piercing.  Solos aside, Neil's drums were just a tad undermixed--perhaps this was intentional as his drumming style can be so dominant.  Overall, the sound level definitely got louder and louder as the concert progressed--several people remarked on it after the show.    Picture wise this was excellent, but with a definite bias towards blue overall.  Flesh tones looked as natural as they can under the \\"limelight\\".  I saw no evidence of edge enhancement or processing, this was probably just directly loaded from the HD cameras without much interference.  Some of the longest shots (and the occasional \\"blimp\\" shot) were of slightly lesser picture quality, but this could have more to do with the slow build-up of smoke inthe venue.  The opening and end credits were of low quality and jagged edges were visible.  The non-concert film clips were pretty much perfect, with some minor smearing during intentionally hokey special effects segments involving the time machine tranformations.    Overall, this was an exciting, involving show that had the theater crowd cheering along with the projected crowd.  The sound started out iffy but steadily improved to very good.  The picture is likely the best we have seen in a Rush concert so far.

1451648537|4.0|This is worth the read, for sure. It gives me more insight into Steve Jobs than I have ever had. But, I think it stopped short. I really wanted to get further inside his head and understand his motivations and how he ticked. Unfortunately, the book is more heavy on Apple and Silicon Valley history than the motivations and inner struggles of the man himself.  I'd gotten the impression from seeing Steve on-screen years ago that he was a self-important jerk (I'm not a follower). This book confirms my suspicions more than I could have ever imagined. Many times the author doesn't condone or sympathize with this behavior. But, at the same time, he does seem to condone or revel in it too sometimes, which is disturbing. Right off the bat, we hear about a very young Steve whose strong-willed and bratty behavior causes those around him (mostly his parents) to \\"bend their wills\\" to Steve's. The author really drives this home, with an almost adoring tone. This eventually gives way to Steve openly revealing that he is much smarter than his parents and \\"special\\", no doubt constantly reinforced by his parents. We all grew up with a kid like this, or may even have one in our own families, the one who thought they were better than everyone else, the one who never was put in there place and realized that they were no more important or \\"special\\" than anyone else. At this point in the book, the author seems really caught up in Steve and doesn't see the behavior for what it is, spoiled and unchecked. This spoiled Young Steve no doubt sets the stage for Adult Steve's continued bad behavior, and I think since he found success so young, he never had to learn to fit in to society like the rest of us do. Fame and fortune seemed to make this excusable. But the author's tone in describing this left a bad taste for me early in the book and caused some eye rolling. (It did get better).  What was here about Steve's entrepreneurial drive and focus on design was fascinating. I really identify withSteve's respect and pursuit of craftsmanship and his focus on aesthetics and design, as well as his burning desire to build a great company. Those same things drive me.  As far as the writing goes, I think if the author had worked at Apple, Jobs would have fired him and said this book \\"sucks\\", as he would often say. I was amazed that the book would flow so well in parts, talking about something in Steve's life then spin 180 degrees in another direction, like Valley history, then Steve's estranged daughter, etc. Pretty scattered. The author also annoyingly refers to Steve's wife by her last name only, \\"Powell\\", throughout most of the book, even after not mentioning her for many pages. Other people get the full name treatment often, but not her. Odd.   All in all, I get the sense that this book was in a draft stage, then was rushed out after Steve died to capitalize on the timing. It's good, but could have been better.

B002VH7P4O|4.0|It's been a two week journey both to get a working apple and then get the FLAC files in shape to hear on my stereo. If you want to know how I did this, read further and I might save you some long and sleepless nights.    1. Getting the Apple  I ordered the little USB apple from Amazon after reading about how great this little thing was. The apple arrived intact, stem and all and I was thrilled. That was short lived however after I found that the USB was defective. Nothing showed up on two computers and two other electronic devices. Amazon refunded the money in a jiffy and I found a used one (the new ones were gone by now) through their warehouse. I had it delivered in two days. When I opened the box, to my horror the little apple box inside the package was smashed in on one side even though the shipping box was fine. (Hmm, UPS reboxed maybe? It was not in a smiley Amazon box) Even though the little black box was badly damaged, the apple inside was fine and this time it worked!    2. Playing on the Computer, iPod  The USB comes with two full sets of music files, mp3 and FLAC. On my PC (Windows XP) I could not use the little button they talk about in the included read me file to transfer the files to iTunes. I had to do this manually. All I had to do my drag the mp3 files into the iTunes library, then transfer them to my iPod. Everything went fine, all is there, album art and all.    As for the FLAC files, they would not play on the computer, but I downloaded Winamp a free program and it plays them no problem. Boy did they sound good on my pair of Bose computer speakers, but that was not enough for me, oh no, I wanted to play them on my stereo [evil laugh here].    3. Playing on my Stereo (Read only if you are brave)  Well there's a lot of technical stuff I slogged through on the internet during my week long journey to figure out how to get these great loss-less files to work on my stereo. If you'd like to take that adventure this is a good place to start: [...]    Without getting into all that mumbo jumbo and trial and error, here is what I did that worked. WARNING! You have to spend MORE MONEY!! (If you are super tech savvy you can probably figure out a free way that is ten times the work)    -Convert your FLAC files to (24 bit-lossless --the same) 48kHz WAV files using dBpoweramp's Audio Conversion software.[...]/ $38 (FLAC files will not play on a DVD or CD player.) Video DVD players play only 48 or 96 kHz, not 44kHz) The FLAC files are 24 bit 44.1 kHz.    -Make an audio VIDEO DVD using Audio DVD Creator. [...]$39.95. CD players will not play 24 bit files. The biggest is 16 bit and it won't sound as good. If you have an AUDIO DVD player you can use AUDIO DVD software. Explained: [...]    4. If all this is too much trouble I think you can buy an NBOX 2nd or 3rd generation media player to connect to your entertainment system. It should play your FLAC files right off the USB. I said should. I don't know for sure. You should research that. NBOX 2nd Gen is around $40 on Amazon. The 3rd is even more.    BOTTOM LINE. Yes, this was a PAIN! But............ When I finally got to hear the white album on my stereo, using my DVD player, I almost cried, the quality was soooooooo good. I have a CD of the same album and it sounds flat in comparison.  The converted FLAC files sound life-like, organic, real! Like others say, you can hear things you've never heard before. You will feel like the Beatles are in the room with you --yes, that good. Well, if you have pretty good speakers.    The four stars instead of five are because of the extreme hassle the USB FLAC files are to play.    You may want to go into the discussions about this product, lots of good info there, and confusing. But if you are gonna get the USB, you really should know what to do with it when you get it.

B004R739LE|4.0|The Lowepro LP36329 Orion DayPack 200 is a vice nice \\"daypack\\" that can be used for carrying a moderate sized photo kit plus some other essentials.  The relatively inexpensive price is a plus.<br /><br />The Orion DayPack 200 is constructed of nylon; there are flaps covering the two main zippers of the bag to lend some protection from rain, but there is no built-in weatherproof cover like in some more expensive backpacks.  And as mentioned in other reviews, there is no \\"quick access\\" feature; you'll need to take off the backpack / unclip / unzip to get to your camera and lenses.<br /><br />(SEE ATTACHED PICTURE)<br /><br />The camera compartment makes up the bottom part of the bag; it is well padded and comes with five dividers which can be used to partition the camera compartment as needed.  I was able to fit my Canon 50D with attached 15-85mm lens, plus a Canon 135L lens, Sigma 50mm f1.4 lens, and Canon 430EX II flash into the compartment and still have a bit of room left over for a spare battery / charger / filter or two.  My only real complaint is that Lowepro was quite optimistic in its specs for the camera compartment.  It states that the interior camera compartment is 6.9\\"H, but my Canon 50D / 15-85mm combo which measured at 6.25\\" high barely fits, and a Canon 70-200 f4L IS at 6.8\\" long will definitely not fit in the camera compartment.  It also would have been nice to have a zipper / Velcro pocket somewhere for filters / memory cards.<br /><br />The top half of the Orion DayPack 200 is basically a large open compartment.  There is a mesh pocket against the back of this that can keep a few items separate; there is some elastic holding the top of this pocket but no zipper.  There is no padding for the top half of the bag, so any lenses carried there (like my 70-200) should be stored in their protective case.  I would also use the top compartment to carry lens hoods, water, and anything else you might need to carry for your day trip.  In addition there are two long but narrow zippered pockets on the outside of the top part of the bag that could be used for keys, cell phones, or other small items; these zippered compartments have no extra protection from the weather.<br /><br />Lastly, the bottom of the bag has two straps that can be used to carry a small tripod, rolled up blanket or jacket, or whatever else you can think of.<br /><br />Overall, I am impressed with the Orion DayPack 200.  It's probably not the correct choice for serious camping / backpacking duty, but it will work fine for carrying a moderate amount of photo gear and other stuff for day trips, and it's price is reasonable considering it's usefulness and quality.<br /><br />EDIT APRIL 2012 - I just used this bag for a trip to the Grand Canyon and Red Rock country in Arizona.  Took a 50D with 24-105L, 430 EX flash, 70-300L, and extra batteries / chargers / etc.  The 50D with 24-105L is a bit tall when stored vertically, but no issues closing the bag and no ill effect to the camera or lens being carried like this.  Everything else fit fine in the bottom compartment, leaving plenty of room in the top for water / snacks / maps.  I agree with other reviews that it would be nice to have a side zipper to get the camera out quicker.  That said I still really like this bag.long but narrow zippered pockets on the outside of the top part of the bag that could be used for keys, cell phones, or other small items; these zippered compartments have no extra protection from the weather.<br /><br />Lastly, the bottom of the bag has two straps that can be used to carry a small tripod, rolled up blanket or jacket, or whatever else you can think of.<br /><br />Overall, I am impressed with the Orion DayPack 200. Nicolas Sparks seems to have the ability to know about all the stages of love, and that it can occur at all ages. Dawson is a strong, flawed, but sympathetic man. He has been searching for over twenty years, but is not certain what he is looking for. Accompanied by a mysterious man that seems to appear when Dawson is in grave danger, he returns to his hometown at the request of the attorney handling the last wishes of Dawson's old friend. The friend had also requested the presence of Amanda, a woman that had been very important to Dawson's past. Together they fulfill the last wishes of Tuck, an elderly man that filled some of the empty spaces in their lives. Dawson's cousins are a foreboding cloud that hangs over what should have been a weekend filled with reminisces, closure, and perhaps hope for their future. Amanda has three children and a husband with an alcohol problem waiting for her back home. And Dawson has to come to grips with an accident that occurred many years before.   The author's love for his home state is shown in the beautifully descriptive phrases he uses to draw us into the story. And he allows us to imagine that we are right there on the porch with Amanda and Dawson, enjoying the flowers that Tuck had planted so lovingly for his wife, Clara. We are also able to comprehend the fear, anxiety, and pain that the cousins bring to everyone that knows them. A little more graphic than the others Sparks has written, it is another 'keeper' for our re-reading pleasure.

I am sure there are discrepancies and errors in the 656 page book. But lets focus on the basics.<br /><br />A successful corporation has to have a visionary.  Jobs certainly was the right man. Most other companies there were chaotic struggle fighting for control of power not to mention executives love to garage sale divisions which took years to formulate. Apple certainly followed Jobs visionary to make it one of the most successful corporations in modern America! Apple CEO needed not to woo Wall Street since it exceeded the projections in all areas. Jobs had the rare opportunity to focus on product details and followed his vision to perfection. Tim Cook certainly was an excellent right operation executive.<br /><br />At IBM, Thomas Watson preached \\"THINK\\" and \\"keep it simple\\". The big blue executives are the first ones to throw them away which had a disastrous impact on the Valley.  THINK and keep it simple are very much what Jobs pursued in his own way.<br /><br />The financial success at Apple was merely a validation of Job's vision and flawless execution of a byproduct. He integrated or improved a neumber of new technologies. Mouse, proprietary operating systems, GUI, Zoom in and features through experimentation. B003VANOFY|4.0|Wireless headsets are still a rare breed with just a handful of options to choose from, but the G930 isn't just profiting from lack of competition. It is an all around great piece of hardware that is better than any headset I've owned in nearly all ways, wireless or not. It has excellent sound and recording quality that isn't impacted by the wireless, as is common with anything using Bluetooth and most of the earlier entries in the wireless headset market.  The three buttons get the job done for navigating through music while you're adventuring in your bathroom or kitchen or whatever manner of illicit walkabout activities you're engaging in (don't go near a running microwave). Having the buttons definitely shows you their usefulness and quickly leads to the desire for a few more. Three buttons is next, previous, and pause which is the minimum amount for it to be of any use and would be greatly serviced by doubling or tripling that. Next version hopefully.  Here's where it loses the star. If it didn't kick such a plethora of asses in other ways it'd lose two stars cause this is a biggy. The joints above the earpieces are really fancy and Robocopeaque and nifty and whatever. They're also structural failure points on a headset widely reported as having a very snug fit. I'm not the most gentle with my stuff but I'm also not club-arming my stuff into walls so I'd label myself in the rough-but-within-reason range.  The elbow joint is designed with a middle small connector swivel piece which is what holds it together, and it bears the brunt of abuse. At the connection points eventually a piece of plastic clamping the three parts of the elbow together will break and you will have an elbow joint which no longer has an elbow. After the elbow is dead the only thing holding it together is the wire. Wires are not load bearing.  I bought these a year ago and around 6 months ago the left side joint had catastrophic failure as I long predicted. I triaged with an electrical tape splint that at least protected the wire and kept it serviceable. The joint is both a socket and a swivel, and the socket will still kind of allow you to keep a solid feeling to it if backed up with enough tape, but you lose all movement obviously and the headset will not fit tightly anymore.  Yesterday the right side failed in the same way. I once again triaged with electrical tape but with both sides gone it's like having giant droopy dog ears that slap against you and fall off if you look down. I triaged with even more electrical tape and created a jury rigged head mount for with a strap for my forehead and another strap that goes behind my head toward the bottom. Between the two ear cups and the straps it once again fits snug as it did before it broke.  It's still an awesome headset otherwise but it became too cumbersome to realistically keep using it. To keep it on my head requires massive amounts of electrical tape and an incredible amount of genius level tape engineering on my part. I can't simply \\"wear\\" it anymore; I have to mount and dismount it from my head like I'm suiting up to pilot a spaceship. It would be cool if I was but I'm not. I'm piloting my chair at my computer and that's not cool normally and especially not with headgear.  Anyway, I ordered a new G930 earlier today. There's no replacement on the market for it yet so my choice is between perfecting my electrical tape head mount or buying a new identical headset. I know I will blow the eblows out of this one too but at least this time I bought 60 yards of heavy duty electrical tape along with it.

0385333846|5.0|In the opening chapter of Slaughterhouse-Five, Kurt Vonnegut describes his service in World War II where, as a prisoner of war, he witnessed the fire bombing that destroyed the German city of Dresden, and his long struggle to write a war novel that reflected what happened. He visits a fellow POW, Bernard O'Hare, looking for a fresh perspective on the event, which he finds from O'Hare's wife. She's openly hostile to the the idea of a war novel. If it's anything like most war stories, she says, Vonnegut will cover up the carnage with glory and cast characters more like John Wayne than the children who really fought and died. Vonnegut promises he will do no such thing, and Slaughterhouse-Five is the fulfilment of that promise.<br /><br />With typical whimsy and dark humor, Vonnegut presents the life of Billy Pilgrim, a well to do optometrist from upstate New York who witnessed the destruction of Dresden as a prisoner of war and, many years later, was abducted by aliens.<br /><br />Billy has also come unstuck in time, unpredictably experiencing different episodes in his life.  The departure from linear time provides Vonnegut the opportunity to alternate between Billy's war experience, the fallout later in life, and his alien abduction. True to Mrs. O'Hare's wishes, Vonnegut's soldiers are young boys who experience war in equal parts horror and absurdity.<br /><br />Our understanding of Billy's struggle with the war - during and after - are revealed mostly through his actions. As Billy is captured and taken to Dresden he trudges along, just trying to stay alive. But Billy's erratic behavior in the years after the war reveal a deep struggle with the horrors experienced. He has a nervous breakdown, troubles his daughter, and becomes rather well known for his lectures on flying saucers and the true nature of time.<br /><br />See, in 1967 Billy was abducted by aliens and kept in a zoo on their home planet, Tralfamadore, for several years. During his abduction, Billy learned that, unlike humans who experience time moment to moment, the Talfamadorian see all things in their temporal entirety; a fixed object from birth to death. Billy goes on to preach this existential perception back on earth, and the reader is left to wonder what consolations it provided Vonnegut.<br /><br />For all the quirky science fiction and dry humor, it is Slaughterhouse-Five's deep sorrow and plea for humanity that lay at its core. It is an inevitable read for anybody invested in good literature, Vonnegut's most essential book, and like nothing else in the canon of great war novels.moment to moment, the Talfamadorian see all things in their temporal entirety; a fixed object from birth to death. Billy goes on to preach this existential perception back on earth, and the reader is left to wonder what consolations it provided Vonnegut.  For all the quirky science fiction and dry humor, it is Slaughterhouse-Five's deep sorrow and plea for humanity that lay at its core. It is an inevitable read for anybody invested in good literature, Vonnegut's most essential book, and like nothing else in the canon of great war novels.

0451169514|3.0|Stephen King has always been a very derrivative writer, and in many respects the 1986 novel IT reflects his own 1982 novella \\"The Body,\\" which is better known as the film STAND BY ME--and perhaps more specifically the 1979 Peter Straub novel GHOST STORY. \\"The Body\\" tells of a group of boys who take a long walk to see the dead body of a dead child, and who must confront their own fears, various frightening accidents, and a gang of vicious bullies; GHOST STORY concerns a group of boys who find themselves terrorized by a shape-shifting spirit who returns years later to further terrorize them as adults. IT is a variation on both, but it casts a somewhat wider net: in this instance the children--six boys and one girl--confront a shape-shifting creature. From time to time this creature appears in the small Maine town of Derry, where it kills children until its thirst is slaked. Thereafter, and under the creature's influence, the town tends to forget that such murders happened ... until the next time.<br /><br />Set in a small, very ordinary town named Derry, the plot of IT is a bit complicated, for it slips back and forth between 1958 and 1985.  In the first, the seven children confront and possibly kill It; in the second the adults those children have become return to Derry when It returns, this time to kill the monster once and for all.  Both stories play out at the same time and both are parallel to each other.  The creature they refer to as It is also a complicated entity, for we are never quite certain of the scope of its powers.  It most often appears in the guise of clown, but It is also able to sense what frightens you the most and assume that appearance if that is more advantageous.  As the novel progresses we find that It is not a ghost, not a demon, not even a shape-shifter in any traditional sense of the term.  It is an inter-dimensional predator, something that might have been inspired by the creations of H.P. Lovecraft, a thing that slips between time and space to attack at will, feeding as much upon the fear it inspires as the actual bodies of its victims.<br /><br />If all this sounds good--well, it is and it isn't.  Over the years King and a host of other writers have made excessive use of child characters in their novels, and the notion was old even in 1986, when the novel was published.  King also has difficulty in making each of the seven characters distinct, particularly in the first portion of the novel, and he has a tendency to tell their stories in a sort of rota system, as if each character has been assigned a number to get a sketch in turn.  As for It, the concept plays out very well until about the last hundred pages of the novels, which define It in a surprisingly prosaic sort of way and which have a confused quality, as if King himself is not entirely sure of what It is supposed to be: monster from another dimenstion, monster from outer space, demi-god of evil, or what.  That said, King, his stories, his characters, and his style have always had a certain crassness about them.  Sometimes it works well, but sometimes it feels out of place, and where IT is concerned the latter applies--occasionally to a point where you might think about putting the novel aside for once and all.<br /><br />Even so, if you're looking for a big, splashy Stephen King horror novel--well, no, it isn't in the same league as 'SALEM'S LOT or even THE SHINING, but the book is still entertaining enough to pull you through one thousand plus pages.  One thing that helps the book be entertaining is the way in which King references his own work (Dick Hallorann from THE SHINING is mentioned, for example), and King takes specific swipes at other horror writers, most particularly Koontz.  This is probably more a book for hardcore King fans than it is for more casual readers, but if you don't expect too much, you'll likely enjoy IT.<br /><br />GFT, Amazon Reviewerack at will, feeding as much upon the fear it inspires as the actual bodies of its victims. B000WGWQG8|5.0|It was not merely *one*  \\"Miracle at Philadelphia,\\" it was a series of miracles - from the explosive \\"Writs of Assistance\\" speech orated by James Otis at Faneuil Hall in Boston that inspired so many of the other Forefathers, to the victory against all odds at Yorktown before the young country to get down to business.  Although the mention of the Pre-Revolution is scant, the very soul of our Nation resonates in this excellent film.  It acknowledges and poignantly presents the personal travail and sacrifices made by these people as they gave their up their lives to the cause, either on the battlefield or in public office, which might be considered the more prolonged and agonizing of the two types of sacrifices.  Finally, the thing that was captured best in this film and which I noted with full understanding,  was the hardest part of all - the bittersweet \\"letting go\\" and returning, bruised and battered,  to the land they loved - the land worth so much of oneself.  It's that dear and familiar \\"something\\" that was lost and found again - the ultimate and beautiful \\"full circle.\\"  But -  resonating through that final freedom, is the fact that while retirement is envisioned as being able to enjoy all of what was at leisure, is, in stark reality, that of losing people you love; children, old friends.  A film for the ages is this one.<br /><br />A truly remarkable set of DVD's, based on the book by David McCullough, as well as many other fine historical accountings of our Revolution for Independence,  it brings a new dimension to the biography of John Adams. Kudos to Tom Hanks and his associates for bringing this tribute to us.  The breathtaking introduction visual of the segmented snake that \\"joins\\" itself and becomes the entire serpent against the billowing new flag is truly moving if you happen to have even a token of emotion for the act of revolution that has given the rest of us so much to build on.  From there, it proceeds to provide us with something the book can't quite manage - the flesh and blood visuals; the living, breathing complexities dwelling in the personalities of the Revolutionists.  The casting was superb.  From the hard-hearted \\" do or die\\" focus of his cousin Samuel Adams, for whom an end justified the means, to the quiet brilliance of Thomas Jefferson; the controlled discipline behind the intelligence of Abigail; the weakness that threatened all but two of his children and their own spouses as they lived with such a strong father; the imposing, regal figure cut by George Washington; the playful mischievousness of Benjamin Franklin who seemed to consider everyone his personal marionettes.  And Adams' own honest, stubborn integrity that refused to bend or break,  starting with the very unpopular defending of the British Officer and his men accused in the Boston Massacre when feeling was running high against the British.  His worth was evident then; that a free people must have equal protection and adequate defense under the laws and it must be from the beginning in order for it to have meaning.<br /><br />It follows Mr. Adam's family as it endures hardship, selflessness, unimaginable tragedy in the loss of not one but two children, lives through war, politics, small pox outbreaks, and manages to not only survive it all, but be instrumental in the building of a nation from the ground up while it was happening, somehow retaining their emotional sanity.  All travel from the back of a horse or in buggies, or the soggy bottoms of leaking ships, it seems impossible now that they could accomplish so much. Time travel that took months, not hours, letter writing bearing news of vital importance that were always in danger of being lost, intercepted, weeks en route when the messages were needed today.  The scene of Henry Knox spilling his substantial weight all over his saddle as his valiant horse trudged along in the mud behind the \\"appropriated\\" cannons from Ticonderoga - and aptly named \\"Liberty and Independence\\" - was worth it's weight in gold.<br /><br />John Adams had a fascination with Thomas Jefferson throughout his life and even into his death, as they died within hours of each other on July 4, 1826, the 50th anniversary of the Independence.  Of Jefferson, he loved, revered, valued his opinions, desired his support in all matters political,felt betrayed by him, broke from him; and in the final \\"circle that remains unbroken\\", renewed his friendship with him at the behest of their wise mutual acquainatence Benjamin Rush, who was the last link between them both. A competition with Jefferson even in death, Adam's last words were about the man who had been such an influence in his own life as they both gave up their lives in service to their new country.  He was also clearly dependent upon the bond he shared with his wife Abigail, a man who was mighty when he had the support of those he respected and loved, but less sure of his own abilities when standing alone against public opinion.<br /><br /> Viewing this film should be inspiration for us all.  In truth, viewing any of these new, beautiful documentaries of the Revolution (there are many good ones) and renewing our appreciation of what we have here, would be timely, in this, an election year preliminary.  A good place to start would be Ken Burns \\"Thomas Jefferson\\", which is equally well made.

0380977273|5.0|Ray Bradbury was the favorite author of my youth. I discovered The Martian Chronicles and Fahrenheit 451 when I was eight or ten and started working my way through everything the library had by Bradbury. This book struck me especially vividly. Perhaps I was just the right age though I never lived in a small town or went to a carnival. The story is so vividly told and so jam packed with wonder and poetry and joy and horror and amazement. Rereading it now some thirty years later, it still thrills and delights. Lasting through time is surely a sign of greatness.  The story is about two boys, Will Holloway and Jim Nightshade, who are best friends in a small Illinois town. One day in late October a lightning rod salesman comes, warns them of a coming storm and gives them a rod to protect Jim's house. That night a mysterious train brings Cooger and Dark's Pandemonium Shadow Show to town. It seems like a benign carnival come far too late in the season, with Halloween approaching. Evil things are afoot as the boys discover the more sinister side of the side shows.  The book is more than an adventure story. It's more than a coming of age story. It's more than a fantastical horror novel. It deals with old age--Will's dad is also a major character who comes to blows with his advanced years (or so he thinks of the 54 he's had) in the same way the boys confront their looming manhood and soon-to-be-lost freedom of boyhood. The story looks at temptation in many forms and the importance of joy in overcoming evil. It is a rich and full novel that definitely rewards multiple readings. I can't wait to share it with my son when he is old enough.

B005WTR4ZI|3.0|Is the book clear? Yes.  Is the book concise? Yes.  Is the book engaging? Yes.  Is the book onto something? Yes.  Is the book well researched? Yes.   Is the book worth reading? Absolutely!  Why on earth did I rate it three stars then?  Stick with me here. Because the book tantalizes with its subtitle that accelerating change is \\"Irreversibly Transforming Employment and the Economy\\" but just when the book gets going, Chapter Four falls flat and feels like an economic recipe for a by-gone era rather than a roadmap to the economy of \\"the digital frontier\\".  As humanity moved from an agrarian society to the pre-industrial and then industrial era, modern economic models took hold that allowed diverse suppliers to rationalize their efforts in a common way and leverage that effort through a common currency.  In short, barter gave way to the abstract concept of a general currency that could be exchanged for goods.    In that process, the machinery of \\"GDP\\" (consumption) and the corporation became all encompassing.  With comparatively archaic tools (in comparison to the machines of 2011), human beings were the primary way of creating products for those same humans to then consume.  As we began to enter the \\"information age\\" in the 80s, 90s and aughts, we created the early incarnations of \\"the digital frontier\\" in the model of the industrial era (i.e. we remade the factory).  Two examples: Software titans copyrighted their work and big think tanks erected barriers to their information to maintain artificial scarcity that aids in keeping prices up and revenue flowing. This is regardless of the fact that information, once created, can be shared and distributed in an essentially frictionless way. Bits and bytes are not bound by the same physical rules as traditional raw material like wood or rare earth elements.  As we decode the machinery of life (DNA, molecular bonds, nanotechnology, etc), the same will be true for physical things...we'll be able to simply reorder material into a form we want. Early generation 3D printers and tissue regeneration are examples of this trend. Yet we continue to bind these information-era processes to economic models of scarcity.    What we need, and what I was hoping this book would help describe, is a different economic model. An economic model in which the concept of abundance, rather than scarcity, is the central driving force.  We have an abundance of people willing to work.  We have an abundance of information. We have an abundance of things that need to be done.  We have an abudance of ability to connect it all together.  We have an increasingly abundant ability to create stuff for virtually free.  We have 7 Billion people on the planet for whom our moral and social compact drive us to provide them with shelter, food, and health care yet we have an economic model that rewards hoarding and selfishness and creating products that produce huge profit margins when equally good, or better, and cheaper solutions exist.  The successful economic model of \\"the digital frontier\\" will decouple supply and demand.  Profit on the supply side will be driven by those who can create the most social value at the lowest cost (housing for free for example). \\"Pay\\" on the demand side will be driven by rewarding human beings to remain active, productive, engaged, and learning rather than trying to \\"race [the] machine\\".  And, no, I don't mean Communism, I mean a capitalistic system with different measures and rewards.   There are numerous alternatives to a GDP-based economic model that have been put forth.  Examples include \\"Gross national happiness\\", \\"Continuum Development Index\\", and the \\"Sharing economy\\" to name just three.  Search for alternatives to GDP for others.  Yet \\"Race Against the Machine\\" did not explore any of these as possible roadmaps to a world where humans' \\"requirement\\" to \\"work\\" becomes obsolete.  With that said, the book serves as an excellent reference for why these trends are happening and why they areirreversible. Everyone should read this book and then help create an economy of abundance!

B005WTR4ZI|5.0|Erik Brynjolfsson and Andrew McAfee have created a powerful, concise and informative discussion of the impact of technology on employment, income distribution and macro economics.  Do not be fooled by the title, Race Against the Machine is not a neo luddite treatise on the evils of automation and technology.  The title is more about generating buzz and attention than an accurate label for what is in this book -- nothing short of the best explanation of the economy we face in the future and the role of technology.<br /><br />This book is highly recommended to anyone who wants to understand why we can have a recession, a jobless recovery and growing income distribution inequities all at the same time.  This book does a tremendous job steering its explanation based on facts, insights from other economists and thought leaders.<br /><br />Brynjolfsson and McAfee's basic argument is that we are just beginning to see the long term impact of technology on the economy. The authors highlight this using the analogy of Chinese story where the emperor agrees to pay a servant a grain of rice and then doubling that amount for each square on the chess board.  That doubling is the foundation of technology's driving forces embodied in the laws of Moore, Metcalfe and others.<br /><br />The authors believe that we are just getting to the back half of the chess board where a doubling of technology creates gigantic leaps in capability at an unprecedented pace.  These leaps are beginning to displace human work as technologies like IBM's Watson and others demonstrate the ability to handle complex work.<br /><br />The book is divided into five chapters;<br /><br />Chapter 1. Technology's influence on the employment and the economy.  The first chapter provides an overview of the book and its chapters.  Here the authors do a good job supporting their argument based on the observation and ideas of others.  The chapter could have been a dry recitation of prior research, but Brynjolfsson and McAfee have described the issue in ways that are broadly accessible.<br /><br />Chapter 2.  Humanity and technology on the second half of the chess board -- discusses the impact of technology on the economy, productivity and employment.  This chapter focuses on things that are emerging as the capability of technology has crossed a threshold.  The most interesting part of this discussion concentrated on General Purpose Technology (GPT) and how these technologies drive further investment in technology.<br /><br />Chapter 3.  Creative destruction: the economic of accelerating technology and disappearing jobs.  This chapter is the most informative as it explains the impact of technology on employment and income  Here the discussion of highly-skilled vs skilled workers, Superstars vs. Everyone else, and Capital vs. Labor all explain different aspects of the economy we all live in.<br /><br />Chapter 4: What is to be done?  Prescriptions and Recommendations -- contains a list of 13 recommendations for fostering organizational innovation and investing in human capital,  These are two areas where investments can lead to creating new employment opportunities and growth,  I will not go over the recommendations, many are ones that you may have heard of in the past, but the authors put these recommendations into a new context.<br /><br />5. Conclusion: the Digital Frontier -- provides a discussion of the new economy emerging and the impact of digitalization in terms of creating new sources of value and disruption.  It is an apt conclusion to the book.<br /><br />Each chapter focuses on a different aspect technology, the economy, employment etc. in a concise and informative way.  The authors take full advantage of the book's electronic form by providing active links to referenced research and opinion.  While not accessible when you are disconnected, the ability to quickly jump into some original source material adds to the value of the book.<br /><br />The book can be easily read in under 4 hours and would be the best use of your time on a long plane ride where you can read and think through the authors ideas and their implications.<br /><br />Brynjolfsson and McAfee have created a readily accessible, influential and provocative book that should be reaUd by business executives, policy makers and technologist to give them a better understanding of the deep forces at work in the global economy,  Some will disagree with the author's recommendations, particularly given that it would be easy to associate some of their recommendations as moderately to the right.  That would be a mistake as I believe Brynjolfsson and McAfee have been able to take a relatively neutral view of an economist to these issues.<br /><br />At $2.99 on Amazon, this book is more than well worth the cost and most importantly your time and attention.hink through the authors ideas and their implications.   Brynjolfsson and McAfee have created a readily accessible, influential and provocative book that should be reaUd by business executives, policy makers and technologist to give them a better understanding of the deep forces at work in the global economy,  Some will disagree with the author's recommendations, particularly given that it would be easy to associate some of their recommendations as moderately to the right.  That would be a mistake as I believe Brynjolfsson and McAfee have been able to take a relatively neutral view of an economist to these issues.   At $2.99 on Amazon, this book is more than well worth the cost and most importantly your time and attention.

This particular version [[ASIN:B00005R87K Nosferatu]], while claiming to be the \\"original silent vampire classic,\\" is actually slightly, yet vitally, edited. One can notice that all of the intertitles have been reworded, and the characters' names have been restored to more accurately reflect this film's relationship to the book by Bram Stoker. For example, Count Orlock is herein know as Count Dracula, Knock is renamed Renfield, etc. Sadly, the editing of the intertitles, while conveying the same (or similar, at least) message as the original ones, removes a lot of the mood-setting and suspense-creating language that helped to make the original so great.  That said, the price point for this particular version is rather lower than others, and is therefore a little bit more accessible.
