1451648537|5.0|This is a gripping journey into the life of an amazing individual.  Despite its girth of nearly 600 pages, the book zips along at a torrid pace.  The interviews with Jobs are fascinating and revealing.  We get a real sense for what it must have been like to be Steve, or to work with him.  That earns the book five stars despite its flaws, in that it's definitely a must-read if you have any interest at all in the subject.  But there are places in the book where I have to say, \\"Huh?\\"  The book is written essentially as a series of stories about Steve.  The book continuously held my interest, but some of the dramas of his life seem muted.  For instance, he came close to going bust when both Next and Pixar were flailing.  There was only the slightest hint that anything dramatic happened in those years.  In one paragraph, Pixar is shown as nearly running him out of money.  A few brief paragraphs later, Toy Story gets released and Jobs' finances are saved for good.  We hear a lot about Tony Fadell's role in the development of iPhone.  Tony led the iPod group and was clearly a major source for the book.  You may know from a recent Businessweek article that Tony was basically driven out of the company shortly after the final introduction of iPhone, due to personality conflicts between him and Scott Forestall, the person now in charge of iOS development.  But the book doesn't say a word about it.  Tony simply disappears from the rest of the book with no explanation, and Forestall is barely mentioned.  Another strange incident was the Jackling house, the house he spent a large part of his life in.  A case could be made that the house is historic simply because Steve spent many of his formative years living in it.  Preservationists were battling with him to save the house.  Only a couple of months before his death, when he must have known he was not going to actually build a house to replace it, he had the house torn down.  I would have loved to learn this story.  Why did he buy it?  Why did he destroy it through neglect?  Why did he acquire such a blind loathing for it that he worked hard to get it torn down?   And why did Jobs keep almost all the Pixar options to himself?  He doesn't seem to have needed the money, or even really wanted it that much.  He could have cut his friends John Lasseter et al into their own huge fortunes.  Lasseter only got about $25 million from Pixar, which seems like a shockingly low amount in view of his contributions.  Now, it's not like they will starve or anything, and I think John can buy pretty much anything he wants, but it still seems surprising Jobs is so ungenerous.  There were a lot of things like this, incidents casually tossed away in a brief paragraph that should have merited an entire chapter.  I think this will always be the best account of the emotional aspects of Steve's life, which are fully covered.  The chapters about his illness moved me to tears.  But as an account of what really happened at Apple and how Steve fixed the company, it's insufficient.  I guess that will have to await more distance from the subject.  Of course what's truly remarkable about Jobs is that he lived a life so full of incident that perhaps no biography has the space to cover the broad sweep of his life.  He accomplished as much as 10 ordinarily famous men.  Maybe the upshot is that you just can't fit a man like this in a book, even if that book's nearly 600 pages.

B005CM9DX6|5.0|Heritage is one of those albums that is going to really split the fans.  It has some of the least accessible material ever done by the band on it and demands a lot from the listener.  This is an album that requires you to come in with an open mind and a love of music in general.  I had somewhat mixed feeling myself on the first listen to it, but the ideas here really grew on me and got me interested in music that were totally new to me.  Heritage is really an appropriate name because I don't think Opeth have ever had the opportunity to express all of their heritage before.  They have usually shown more of the sides of themselves that are easiest to grasp and left the obscure in the background as subtle aftertastes.  Those are the elements of Opeth that this album focuses on.  This album is definitely not a Damnation sequel or like anything else they have done.  It sounds more like Yes or King Crimson except twisted and evil.  I'd even say this is darker in tone than Watershed was.  If you weren't convinced before, Opeth proves here that you don't need death growls or insane distortion to sound heavy.  The atmosphere will draw you in and bring you into a world that, while dark and evil, still feels eerily human and familiar.  The subtlety in every song creates music that is truly special and worth many more listens.  I love this album, but it isn't perfect.  Transitions in some songs(Haxprocess especially) can be too awkward at times and I have a pretty high tolerance for that sort of thing.  There are a few examples of lyrics that put a sour taste in your mouth, which is weird because Opeth's lyrics are usually one of their strengths.  \\"Hey you, will you be true\\"... I know you can do better than that Mikael!  For these reasons and what the album demands of you, I wouldn't recommend this to a new listener of the band.  If you are trying to get into Opeth for the first time, Blackwater Park is an album that will give you a good taste of the many sides of the band.  Overall though, Heritage is an amazing experience.  Many bands tend to write in cycles.  If Still Life was what led to Blackwater Park, I think Heritage is going to lead to an album that has the same kind of impact.
B0053YGYO4|3.0|The quick verdict - disappointing.  Sure, \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" is a good pop/rock album; but for a band with so much potential, this effort lacks the strong choruses needed to propel their ideas to the next level.  The vocal lines are placed second in the priority list here; so when Chris Martin proclaims \\"It Hurts Like Heaven,\\" the listener merely recognizes that song's title but fails to connect with it in any substantial way.  And so goes the whole album.  Whereas 2008's \\"Viva La Vida (Or Death And All His Friends)\\" was ambitious in that it experimented with world music and hints of dream pop, \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" feels like a half-hearted attempt to recapture the sound from the outstanding \\"X & Y\\" (2005) with loads of 80's synth-pop and splashes of electronica.    The problem with this project(?) - it is content to be simply good, not great.  For a band that has embraced the `unfair' comparisons to U2 at times, aiming for mediocrity will not achieve them superstar status.  While the absurd Coldplay vs. U2 debate on the internet continues, it doesn't help Coldplay fans' case when U2 gave the world \\"The Joshua Tree\\" as their fifth studio album - meanwhile, Coldplay's fifth effort is this weirdly titled lukewarm pop album.  There is no comparison!  At this point, Coldplay seems destined to be like The Doobie Brothers, America and The Steve Miller Band in that those classic rock acts are remembered far more for their greatest hits albums than their original studio records.  It is downright odd that fellow Brit-rock band Embrace (which was ironically aided by Coldplay in their success) have a masterpiece in their fourth album called \\"Out Of Nothing\\" (2004), yet the world still waits for Coldplay to equal such a feat.  While some regard \\"A Rush Of Blood To The Head\\" as brilliant; in truth, it is an average album that captures a band in transition and is quite uneven.    With the albums \\"Viva La Vida\\" and \\"Mylo Xyloto,\\" one has to question their decision making process.  Example One - the disastrous use of paired and hidden tracks on the former project.  Why was \\"Chinese Sleep Chant\\" (which was the most exciting track) hidden behind \\"Yes\\" (which was the dullest track)?  Why wasn't the lovely \\"Reign Of Love\\" given its own track number instead of being paired with the popular \\"Lovers In Japan?\\"  \\"Chinese Sleep Chant\\" would have been an incredible opening track; meanwhile, \\"Reign Of Love\\" ends up being forgotten as some kind of reprise to another song.  To make matters worse, their 2008 album fumbles its finale by adding yet another hidden track after the powerful \\"Death And All His Friends.\\"  It is the equivalent of suppressing a touchdown celebration until referees have taken three minutes to determine whether a player did in fact score or not.    Example Two  -  the unnecessary introduction tracks on \\"Mylo Xyloto.\\"  Why is the track \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" separate from \\"Hurts Like Heaven?\\"  Why is the track \\"M.M.I.X.\\" separate from \\"Every Teardrop Is A Waterfall?\\"  Why is the track \\"A Hopeful Transmission\\" separate from \\"Don't Let It Break Your Heart?\\"  Instead of six tracks, it should only be three.  The band appears to be overly conscious of how fans view their material and there is an implied concern that audiences have little patience these days for embellished introductions.  Using this methodology, U2 would've cut \\"Where The Streets Have No Name\\" into two parts by making the first 1:46 of that classic song its own track.  Thankfully, that didn't happen!  The real question for Coldplay is - why bring in Brian Eno to add his distinct ambient rock sensibilities and then make it so easy for listeners to skip over those tracks to the more accessible (and mainstream) songs?  This inexplicable approach undermines all their work.    Make no mistake though - the band is clearly comfortable in its own skin more so than the last outing.  \\"Mylo Xyloto\\" is a pleasant sugary dose of optimistic candy.  While the potential is still there for Coldplay to deliver us their grand masterpiece; for now, solid glossy pop will have to do.  The most notable tracks here are:  \\"Every Teardrop Is A Waterfall,\\" \\"Charlie Brown,\\" \\"Up With The Birds\\" and \\"Don't Let It Break Your Heart.\\"  Hopefully next time, the album's title will be something less strange as combining the name of a Scottish electronica artist with a fictionalized instrument - xylophone toes.  Mylo's debut album was called \\"Destroy Rock & Roll\\" incidentally.  Does this mean more pop and less rock from Coldplay in the future?  If it's honest and interesting, then I'll be fine with wherever they go next.

1451629184|2.0|With no disrespect intended, in terms of the story and tragedy that occurred, this book is lacking. Granted, this woman is amazing having survived an ordeal that would have likely killed the masses, it falls short. Duggard is writing the book from a 5th grade level - the age in which she was kidnapped. The book often feels like a child is narrating and falls short on the notion of how an adult would have seen the situation. After each chapter there is a \\"reflection\\" which gives hope that Duggard will revamp the story from the standpoint of an adult with more intelligence but often she is still just the confused little girl in the body of a 31 year old. Now, I was forewarned the story would be like this via the introduction where she self-admits the book is a work in progress, much like her own life. A large portion of the book is dedicated to poems and ranting questions on loneliness. Much of the book focuses on her want for her mother, but it is Duggard herself who paints the woman to be less than mother of the year. The first chapter opens with her mother falling short on a promise to kiss her good morning, an 11 year old making her own school lunch and being trapped with a stepfather who openly hates her. How can Duggard want for a woman so badly who seems so disconnected to her child? Duggard is a cat lover and even that statement is one understated. There are tales of probably 20 different cats she encounters and how they are the #1 thing in her life, aside from the interjection of the daughters she has birthed. About halfway through the book you will get quite sick of open-ended and unanswered questions in regards to loneliness (isn't the point of this book to answer our question and not create more?) and how important her animals are to her - arguably to keep her sane in the situation. As a reader you want to scream at her to run, kick and fight rather than do just that for her sick kittens and not the daughters that were fathered and are being raised by an accused child molester and rapist. As a graduate psychology student I enjoyed the book for an in-depth look on what Jaycee herself lived through. I picked up the book looking for the sordid little details the media left out and felt quite disappointed. Duggard's details are in her animals and of course, stricken with Stockholm syndrome, her fascination with her captor. In one chapter she casually jumps to a story talking about how she is permitted to leave the residence she has been chained to after years of being tortured! You would think this would be a climax of the book but is rather overlooked and quickly told, leaving us wanting more. Even the story of her being \\"discovered\\" is quite dull and I found myself more intrigued with an essay I came across on wikipedia rather than this 280+ page book. If you followed this story in the media like I did, save yourself the time of reading this book - you already have more details from the MSNBC report than you will find in here. One of Duggard's goals upon escaping was to become a best selling author. Congrats, Duggard, you did it. And while the story is deserving of such a title your ability to write is not.

B000E1ZBGS|5.0|It's always a challenge to successfully adapt a beloved classic novel into a film, maintaining the plot and spirit of the story. Joe Wright's Pride & Prejudice accomplished this amazingly well. Aside from a few necessary omissions due to time constraints (and yes, a couple of small changes - which I think worked for the better on screen), this version adheres to the original story faithfully. The casting and acting was superior on all accounts. Keira Knightly and Matthew MacFadyen have scorching hot chemistry. I am typically not a huge fan of Knightly, but I think she took on the character of Lizzie superbly. Brenda Blethyn had me laughing out loud at her portrayal of Mrs. Bennet. Donald Sutherland is very touching in his role as Mr. Bennet, and the actresses rounding out the Bennet family performed flawlessly, even though two of them (Talulah Riley and Carey Mulligan) had practically no acting experience before this film was made. I don't even need to mention Judi Dench - we all know she is one hell of an actress.  Beyond the story and characters, the cinemetography is absolutely breathtaking. This film is like a painting come to life. You can freeze-frame almost any shot in this entire film, put it on canvas, and hang it on your wall. It's THAT good. This film is a feast for the eyes if there ever was one. Hats off to Joe Wright and the entire crew for their work of art in motion.
